## Chapter: Thermodynamic Equilibrium



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of thermodynamic equilibrium. Thermodynamic equilibrium is a fundamental concept in the study of thermodynamics and kinetics, and it plays a crucial role in understanding how systems behave under different conditions. 



Thermodynamic equilibrium is a state of a system where all the macroscopic properties such as pressure, temperature, and volume are unchanging in time. It is a state where all the driving forces within a system are balanced, and no spontaneous changes occur. This concept is central to the understanding of how energy is transferred and transformed in a system.



We will explore the conditions necessary for a system to reach thermodynamic equilibrium and the factors that can influence this state. We will also discuss the concept of reversibility and irreversibility in thermodynamic processes and how these concepts relate to the equilibrium state. 



Furthermore, we will delve into the mathematical representations of thermodynamic equilibrium. For instance, we will discuss the Gibbs free energy, represented as `$\Delta G$`, and its significance in predicting the spontaneity of a reaction. We will also explore the concept of equilibrium constants and how they are used to quantify the extent of a reaction.



By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of thermodynamic equilibrium and its implications in various physical and chemical processes. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will explore more complex thermodynamic and kinetic phenomena.



So, let's embark on this journey to unravel the intricacies of thermodynamic equilibrium and its profound implications in the realm of thermodynamics and kinetics.



### Section: 16.1 Definition of Thermodynamic Equilibrium:



#### 16.1a Introduction to Thermodynamic Equilibrium



Thermodynamic equilibrium is a state of balance where all the macroscopic properties of a system, such as pressure, temperature, and volume, are unchanging with time. This state is achieved when all the driving forces within a system are balanced, and no spontaneous changes occur. In other words, a system in thermodynamic equilibrium is in a state of maximum entropy, where energy is evenly distributed, and there is no net flow of matter or energy.



In the context of chemical reactions, thermodynamic equilibrium refers to the state where the rate of the forward reaction equals the rate of the reverse reaction, resulting in no net change in the concentrations of reactants and products. This state is characterized by a constant Gibbs free energy, which is a measure of the maximum reversible work a thermodynamic system can perform at constant temperature and pressure.



The concept of thermodynamic equilibrium is central to the study of thermodynamics and kinetics, as it provides a framework for understanding how systems behave under different conditions. It also serves as a foundation for the study of more complex phenomena, such as phase transitions, chemical reactions, and transport processes.



The mathematical representation of thermodynamic equilibrium involves the minimization of the Gibbs free energy, subject to certain constraints. This is a standard problem in optimization, known as constrained minimization, and it can be solved using various methods, such as the method of Lagrange multipliers.



The Gibbs free energy at equilibrium can be expressed as:


$$

G = \sum_{j} \mu_{j} N_{j}

$$


where $\mu_{j}$ is the chemical potential of molecular species "j", and $N_{j}$ is the amount of molecular species "j". The chemical potential can be expressed in terms of thermodynamic activity as:


$$

\mu_{j} = \mu_{j}^{\ominus} + RT \ln A_{j}

$$


where $\mu_{j}^{\ominus}$ is the chemical potential in the standard state, "R" is the gas constant, "T" is the absolute temperature, and $A_{j}$ is the activity.



For a closed system, the total number of atoms of each element remains constant, which leads to the constraints:


$$

\sum_{j} a_{ij} N_{j} = b_{i}

$$


where $a_{ij}$ is the number of atoms of element "i" in molecule "j" and $b_{i}$ is the total number of atoms of element "i". 



The equilibrium condition is given by:


$$

\mu_{j} + \sum_{i} \lambda_{i} a_{ij} = 0

$$


where the $\lambda_{i}$ are the Lagrange multipliers, one for each element. This is a set of equations that can be solved for the equilibrium concentrations $N_{j}$, as long as the chemical activities are known.



In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the mathematical representations of thermodynamic equilibrium and explore its implications in various physical and chemical processes.



#### 16.1b Characteristics of Thermodynamic Equilibrium



Thermodynamic equilibrium is characterized by several key features that distinguish it from other states of a system. These characteristics are derived from the fundamental definition of thermodynamic equilibrium as a state of maximum entropy and minimum Gibbs free energy.



1. **Unchanging Macroscopic Properties:** In thermodynamic equilibrium, all macroscopic properties of the system, such as pressure, temperature, and volume, remain constant over time. This is because all the driving forces within the system are balanced, and there is no net flow of matter or energy.



2. **Constant Chemical Potential:** At equilibrium, the chemical potential of each molecular species in the system is constant. The chemical potential, denoted by $\mu_{j}$, is a measure of the 'free' or 'available' energy of a molecular species "j". It can be expressed in terms of thermodynamic activity as:



    $$

    \mu_{j} = \mu_{j}^{\ominus} + RT \ln A_{j}

    $$



    where $\mu_{j}^{\ominus}$ is the chemical potential in the standard state, "R" is the gas constant, "T" is the absolute temperature, and "A_{j}$" is the activity of the molecular species "j".



3. **Minimization of Gibbs Free Energy:** The state of thermodynamic equilibrium is characterized by the minimization of the Gibbs free energy "G", which is a measure of the maximum reversible work a thermodynamic system can perform at constant temperature and pressure. At equilibrium, the Gibbs free energy is at a minimum, and can be expressed as:



    $$

    G = \sum_{j} \mu_{j} N_{j}

    $$



    where $\mu_{j}$ is the chemical potential of molecular species "j", and $N_{j}$ is the amount of molecular species "j".



4. **Constrained Minimization:** The minimization of the Gibbs free energy at equilibrium is subject to certain constraints. For a closed system, no particles may enter or leave, although they may combine in various ways. The total number of atoms of each element will remain constant. This leads to a standard problem in optimization known as constrained minimization, which can be solved using various methods, such as the method of Lagrange multipliers.



Understanding these characteristics of thermodynamic equilibrium is crucial for studying and predicting the behavior of thermodynamic systems under different conditions. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the mathematical representation of thermodynamic equilibrium and explore how it can be used to analyze various thermodynamic processes.



#### 16.1c Role in Thermodynamics



The concept of thermodynamic equilibrium plays a pivotal role in the field of thermodynamics. It serves as the foundation for understanding the behavior of systems and the changes they undergo. The principles of thermodynamics are built upon the assumption that systems naturally evolve towards a state of equilibrium.



1. **Second Law of Thermodynamics:** The second law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of an isolated system always increases or remains constant; it never decreases. This law implies that systems naturally evolve towards a state of maximum entropy, which is the state of thermodynamic equilibrium. In other words, a system will spontaneously move towards equilibrium, and once it reaches this state, it will remain there unless acted upon by an external force.



2. **Phase Equilibrium:** The concept of thermodynamic equilibrium is crucial in understanding phase transitions. At phase equilibrium, the chemical potential of a substance is the same in all phases, and there is no net flow of matter between phases. This is the principle behind phenomena such as the boiling and freezing of water.



3. **Chemical Equilibrium:** In chemical reactions, thermodynamic equilibrium occurs when the rates of the forward and reverse reactions are equal, resulting in no net change in the concentrations of the reactants and products. The equilibrium constant, which is derived from the Gibbs free energy of the reaction, provides a quantitative measure of the position of the equilibrium.



4. **Thermal Equilibrium:** Two systems are in thermal equilibrium if they are in contact with each other and there is no net flow of heat between them. This is the principle behind the zeroth law of thermodynamics, which states that if two systems are each in thermal equilibrium with a third system, then they are in thermal equilibrium with each other.



5. **Mechanical Equilibrium:** A system is in mechanical equilibrium when there is no net force acting on it. This is the principle behind the equilibrium of forces in statics and dynamics.



In conclusion, the concept of thermodynamic equilibrium is fundamental to the understanding of thermodynamics and its applications. It provides a framework for analyzing and predicting the behavior of systems, from simple mechanical systems to complex chemical reactions.



#### 16.2a Definition of Phase Equilibrium



Phase equilibrium is a state of a system where the properties of different phases coexisting in the system are unchanging with time. This state is achieved when the chemical potential of a substance is the same in all phases, resulting in no net flow of matter between phases. This is the principle behind phenomena such as the boiling and freezing of water, where the liquid and gas phases (in the case of boiling) or the liquid and solid phases (in the case of freezing) are in equilibrium with each other.



The phase rule, formulated by Josiah Willard Gibbs, provides a mathematical framework to understand phase equilibrium. The phase rule equation is given by:


$$

F = C - P + 2

$$


where:

- $F$ is the number of degrees of freedom, i.e., the number of independent variables that can be changed without affecting the number of phases in equilibrium.

- $C$ is the number of components, i.e., the chemically independent constituents of the system.

- $P$ is the number of phases, i.e., the physically distinct and mechanically separable portions of the system.



The phase rule equation tells us that the number of degrees of freedom is equal to the number of components minus the number of phases plus two (the two extra degrees of freedom correspond to temperature and pressure). This equation is valid provided the equilibrium between phases is not influenced by gravitational, electrical or magnetic forces, or by surface area, and only by temperature, pressure, and concentration.



In the context of phase equilibrium, the degrees of freedom represent the number of intensive variables (such as temperature, pressure, or composition) that can be independently varied without changing the number of phases in equilibrium. For example, in a single-component system (like pure water), at the triple point where all three phases (solid, liquid, and gas) coexist, there are no degrees of freedom; both temperature and pressure are fixed.



Understanding phase equilibrium and the phase rule is crucial for many applications in engineering and science, such as materials science, chemical engineering, and earth sciences, where phase transformations play a key role.



#### 16.2b Characteristics of Phase Equilibrium



Phase equilibrium is characterized by several key features that are a direct result of the fundamental laws of thermodynamics. These characteristics are crucial in understanding the behavior of systems at phase equilibrium.



1. **Uniformity of Temperature:** At phase equilibrium, the temperature is uniform throughout all phases. This is a direct consequence of the zeroth law of thermodynamics, which states that if two systems are each in thermal equilibrium with a third system, they are in thermal equilibrium with each other. In the context of phase equilibrium, this means that if two phases are in equilibrium with each other, they must be at the same temperature.



2. **Uniformity of Pressure:** Similarly, the pressure is uniform across all phases at equilibrium. This is due to the mechanical equilibrium condition, which states that the pressure must be the same in all parts of a system in mechanical equilibrium. In a system with multiple phases, this means that the pressure is the same in all phases.



3. **Equality of Chemical Potential:** The chemical potential of a substance, which is a measure of the 'chemical drive' or tendency of a substance to change, is the same in all phases at equilibrium. This is a result of the second law of thermodynamics, which states that a system will tend towards a state of minimum free energy. In the context of phase equilibrium, this means that the system will adjust itself such that the chemical potential of a substance is the same in all phases, as any difference would provide a 'drive' for the system to change.



4. **Constant Composition:** At phase equilibrium, the composition of each phase remains constant over time. This is because any change in composition would result in a change in the chemical potential, driving the system away from equilibrium.



These characteristics of phase equilibrium are fundamental to understanding the behavior of systems at equilibrium. They provide the basis for the phase rule and other tools used to analyze phase equilibrium, and are crucial in applications ranging from materials science to chemical engineering.



In the next section, we will explore the concept of phase diagrams, which provide a graphical representation of the phase behavior of a system as a function of temperature, pressure, and composition. These diagrams are a powerful tool for visualizing and predicting the behavior of systems at phase equilibrium.



#### 16.2c Role in Thermodynamics



The phase equilibrium plays a significant role in thermodynamics, particularly in the understanding of heat transfer, entropy production, and the behavior of systems at equilibrium. 



1. **Heat Transfer:** The uniformity of temperature at phase equilibrium, as discussed in the previous section, is a crucial factor in heat transfer. According to the general equation of heat transfer, the rate of heat transfer is directly proportional to the temperature difference between two phases. At phase equilibrium, since the temperature is uniform across all phases, there is no net heat transfer between the phases. This is a direct consequence of the zeroth law of thermodynamics.



2. **Entropy Production:** The entropy production in a system at phase equilibrium is also of great importance. The equation for entropy production, given by 



    $$

    \rho T \frac{Ds}{Dt} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + \frac{\mu}{2}\left( \frac{\partial v_{i}}{\partial x_{j}} + \frac{\partial v_{j}}{\partial x_{i}} - \frac{2}{3}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}

    $$



    shows that in the absence of thermal conduction and viscous forces, the entropy production collapses to $Ds/Dt=0$, indicating that ideal fluid flow is isentropic. This is a direct consequence of the second law of thermodynamics, which states that in an isolated system, the entropy can only increase or remain constant. At phase equilibrium, the entropy remains constant, indicating that the system is in a state of maximum entropy.



3. **Behavior of Systems at Equilibrium:** The characteristics of phase equilibrium, such as uniformity of temperature and pressure, equality of chemical potential, and constant composition, provide a framework for understanding the behavior of systems at equilibrium. These characteristics are a direct result of the fundamental laws of thermodynamics and provide a basis for the analysis of systems in various fields, from engineering to environmental science.



In conclusion, phase equilibrium is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that provides a deep understanding of the behavior of systems at equilibrium. It is crucial in the study of heat transfer, entropy production, and the general behavior of systems at equilibrium.



#### 16.3a Definition of Chemical Equilibrium



Chemical equilibrium is a state in a chemical reaction where the concentrations of the reactants and products have become constant due to the forward and reverse reactions occurring at the same rate. This does not imply that the amounts of reactants and products are equal, but rather that their ratios remain constant. 



The concept of chemical equilibrium is closely tied to the Gibbs free energy change, $\Delta G$, and the equilibrium constant, $K$. The Gibbs free energy change is a measure of the maximum reversible work that a thermodynamic system can perform at constant temperature and pressure. It is given by the equation:


$$

\Delta G = \Delta H - T\Delta S

$$


where $\Delta H$ is the change in enthalpy, $T$ is the absolute temperature, and $\Delta S$ is the change in entropy. 



The equilibrium constant, $K$, is a measure of the ratio of the concentrations of the products to the reactants at equilibrium, each raised to the power of their stoichiometric coefficients. It is given by the equation:


$$

K = \frac{[C]^c[D]^d}{[A]^a[B]^b}

$$


where $[A]$, $[B]$, $[C]$, and $[D]$ are the molar concentrations of the reactants and products, and $a$, $b$, $c$, and $d$ are their respective stoichiometric coefficients.



The relationship between the Gibbs free energy change and the equilibrium constant is given by the equation:


$$

\Delta G^o = -RT \ln K

$$


where $R$ is the gas constant, $T$ is the absolute temperature, and $K$ is the equilibrium constant. This equation shows that the Gibbs free energy change is negative for spontaneous reactions (those that proceed to the right), and positive for non-spontaneous reactions (those that proceed to the left). 



In the context of chemical equilibrium, the chemical potential, $\mu_i$, of the $i$th species in a chemical reaction is the partial derivative of the free energy with respect to the number of moles of that species, $N_i$. The chemical potential can be calculated in terms of its activity, $a_i$, using the equation:


$$

\mu_i = \mu_i^o + RT \ln a_i

$$


where $\mu_i^o$ is the standard chemical potential of the species, $R$ is the gas constant, $T$ is the temperature, and $a_i$ is the activity of the species. 



In practice, concentrations are more useful than activities. Activities can be calculated from concentrations if the activity coefficients are known, but this is rarely the case. If activity coefficients are unknown, they may be subsumed into the equilibrium constant, which becomes a concentration quotient.



#### 16.3b Characteristics of Chemical Equilibrium



Chemical equilibrium is characterized by several key features, which are derived from the principles of thermodynamics and kinetics. 



1. **Dynamic Nature:** Chemical equilibrium is a dynamic state, not a static one. Even though the concentrations of the reactants and products remain constant, the forward and reverse reactions continue to occur. This is due to the fact that the rate of the forward reaction equals the rate of the reverse reaction, resulting in no net change in the concentrations of the reactants and products.



2. **Dependence on Initial Concentrations:** The position of the equilibrium, i.e., the relative concentrations of the reactants and products at equilibrium, depends on the initial concentrations of the reactants and products. However, the value of the equilibrium constant $K$ is independent of the initial concentrations.



3. **Dependence on Temperature:** The position of the equilibrium and the value of the equilibrium constant $K$ both depend on the temperature. According to the Van't Hoff equation:



    $$

    \frac{d \ln K}{dT} = \frac{\Delta H^o}{RT^2}

    $$



    where $\Delta H^o$ is the standard enthalpy change for the reaction, $R$ is the gas constant, and $T$ is the absolute temperature. This equation shows that an increase in temperature will shift the equilibrium towards the endothermic direction (where $\Delta H^o > 0$), and a decrease in temperature will shift the equilibrium towards the exothermic direction (where $\Delta H^o < 0$).



4. **Minimization of Gibbs Energy:** At equilibrium, the Gibbs free energy $G$ of the system is at a minimum. This is due to the fact that the chemical potential $\mu_j$ of each species $j$ in the reaction is constant, and the total Gibbs free energy of the system, which is the sum of the chemical potentials multiplied by the amounts of the species, is minimized. This can be expressed mathematically as:



    $$

    G = \sum_j \mu_j N_j

    $$



    where $N_j$ is the amount of species $j$. The minimization of Gibbs energy is subject to the constraint that the total number of atoms of each element remains constant in a closed system.



5. **Influence of External Factors:** The position of the equilibrium can be influenced by external factors such as pressure and the presence of a catalyst. According to Le Chatelier's principle, if a system at equilibrium is subjected to a change in pressure, temperature, or concentration of a component, the system will adjust itself to counteract the effect of the change and restore the equilibrium. A catalyst, on the other hand, does not change the position of the equilibrium or the value of the equilibrium constant $K$, but it increases the rate at which the equilibrium is achieved by lowering the activation energy for both the forward and reverse reactions.



These characteristics of chemical equilibrium provide a comprehensive understanding of the behavior of chemical reactions and the factors that influence them. They form the basis for the quantitative analysis of chemical reactions and the prediction of their outcomes under different conditions.



#### 16.3c Role in Thermodynamics



Chemical equilibrium plays a significant role in thermodynamics, particularly in the context of entropy and the second law of thermodynamics. 



The second law of thermodynamics states that the total entropy of an isolated system can never decrease over time, and is constant if and only if all processes are reversible. Isolated systems spontaneously evolve towards thermodynamic equilibrium, the state with maximum entropy.



In the context of chemical reactions, this law implies that a system will naturally evolve towards a state of chemical equilibrium, where the entropy of the system is maximized. This is because at equilibrium, the system has reached a state of maximum randomness or disorder, which corresponds to a maximum in entropy.



The entropy change for a chemical reaction can be calculated using the equation:


$$

\Delta S = \int_{i}^{f} \frac{dQ_{rev}}{T}

$$


where $dQ_{rev}$ is the infinitesimal amount of heat absorbed from the surroundings in a reversible process, and $T$ is the absolute temperature. 



The entropy change for a reaction provides insight into the spontaneity of the reaction. If $\Delta S > 0$, the reaction is spontaneous as written at constant temperature and pressure. If $\Delta S < 0$, the reaction is non-spontaneous as written, but the reverse reaction is spontaneous. If $\Delta S = 0$, the system is at equilibrium and there is no net change.



The concept of chemical equilibrium is also closely tied to the Gibbs free energy, $G$, of a system. At equilibrium, the Gibbs free energy of the system is at a minimum. This is due to the fact that the chemical potential $\mu_j$ of each species $j$ in the reaction is constant, and the total Gibbs free energy of the system, which is the sum of the chemical potentials multiplied by the amounts of the species, is minimized. This can be expressed mathematically as:


$$

G = \sum_j \mu_j N_j

$$


where $N_j$ is the amount of species $j$. 



In conclusion, the concept of chemical equilibrium is a cornerstone in the study of thermodynamics, providing a link between the macroscopic properties of a system (such as temperature and pressure) and its microscopic properties (such as the concentrations of different species).



#### 16.4a Definition of Thermal Equilibrium



Thermal equilibrium is a state of balance between two or more systems in which there is no net flow of thermal energy from one system to another. This state is achieved when all participating systems have the same temperature. 



In the context of thermodynamics, thermal equilibrium is a specific type of equilibrium that is concerned with temperature and heat transfer. When two systems are in thermal equilibrium, they are at the same temperature and no heat flows between them. This is a direct consequence of the zeroth law of thermodynamics, which states that if two systems are each in thermal equilibrium with a third system, then they are in thermal equilibrium with each other.



Mathematically, if we have three systems A, B, and C, and if A is in thermal equilibrium with B, and B is in thermal equilibrium with C, then A is in thermal equilibrium with C. This can be represented as:


$$

\text{If } T_A = T_B \text{ and } T_B = T_C \text{, then } T_A = T_C

$$


where $T_A$, $T_B$, and $T_C$ are the temperatures of systems A, B, and C, respectively.



It is important to note that thermal equilibrium is a necessary but not sufficient condition for thermodynamic equilibrium. While thermal equilibrium only requires the equality of temperatures, thermodynamic equilibrium requires the equality of temperatures, pressures, and chemical potentials. 



In other words, two systems in thermal equilibrium have equal temperatures, but they may not be in thermodynamic equilibrium if their pressures or chemical potentials are different. This distinction is crucial in understanding the broader concept of thermodynamic equilibrium, which encompasses not only thermal equilibrium but also mechanical and chemical equilibrium.



In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of thermal equilibrium and explore its implications in thermodynamics.



#### 16.4b Characteristics of Thermal Equilibrium



Thermal equilibrium is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics, and it has several key characteristics that are important to understand. 



1. **No Net Heat Flow**: The most defining characteristic of thermal equilibrium is that there is no net flow of heat between the systems in equilibrium. This means that the amount of heat leaving one system is equal to the amount of heat entering the other system. This is a direct consequence of the zeroth law of thermodynamics, which states that if two systems are in thermal equilibrium with a third system, they are in thermal equilibrium with each other. 



2. **Equal Temperatures**: Another key characteristic of thermal equilibrium is that all systems in thermal equilibrium have the same temperature. This is because temperature is a measure of the average kinetic energy of the particles in a system, and in thermal equilibrium, the average kinetic energy of all systems is the same. This can be represented mathematically as:



    $$

    T_A = T_B = T_C

    $$



    where $T_A$, $T_B$, and $T_C$ are the temperatures of systems A, B, and C, respectively.



3. **Time Invariance**: Thermal equilibrium is a state that does not change with time. Once a system reaches thermal equilibrium, it will remain in that state until an external force or energy is applied to it. This is because the laws of thermodynamics are time-invariant, meaning they do not change over time.



4. **Isolated Systems**: Thermal equilibrium is typically discussed in the context of isolated systems. An isolated system is one in which no matter or energy can enter or leave. In reality, perfect isolation is impossible to achieve, but the concept is useful for understanding the principles of thermodynamics.



5. **Entropy Maximization**: In thermal equilibrium, the entropy of the system is maximized. Entropy is a measure of the disorder or randomness of a system, and it tends to increase over time. When a system reaches thermal equilibrium, it has reached a state of maximum entropy, and any further changes would decrease the entropy, which is not possible according to the second law of thermodynamics.



In the next section, we will discuss the concept of thermal equilibrium in the context of heat transfer and the laws of thermodynamics. We will also explore how thermal equilibrium is achieved and how it can be disturbed.



#### 16.4c Role in Thermodynamics



The concept of thermal equilibrium plays a crucial role in thermodynamics, particularly in the understanding of heat transfer, entropy production, and the behavior of systems at equilibrium. 



**Heat Transfer**: In the context of heat transfer, thermal equilibrium is the state at which the rate of heat transfer between two systems is zero. This is because the temperatures of the two systems are equal, and hence, there is no temperature gradient to drive the heat transfer. This can be represented by the general equation of heat transfer:


$$

q = -k \nabla T

$$


where $q$ is the heat transfer rate, $k$ is the thermal conductivity, and $\nabla T$ is the temperature gradient. At thermal equilibrium, $\nabla T = 0$, and hence, $q = 0$.



**Entropy Production**: The concept of thermal equilibrium is also central to the understanding of entropy production. As mentioned in the previous section, the entropy of a system is maximized at thermal equilibrium. This can be represented by the equation for entropy production:


$$

\rho T \frac{Ds}{Dt} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + \frac{\mu}{2}\left( \frac{\partial v_{i}}{\partial x_{j}} + \frac{\partial v_{j}}{\partial x_{i}} - \frac{2}{3}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}

$$


In the absence of thermal conduction and viscous forces, the equation for entropy production simplifies to $Ds/Dt=0$, indicating that the flow of an ideal fluid is isentropic.



**Behavior of Systems at Equilibrium**: Thermal equilibrium also provides insights into the behavior of systems at equilibrium. For instance, the vapor pressures of elements at thermal equilibrium can be used to understand phase transitions. Similarly, the concept of thermal equilibrium is crucial in understanding the operation of engines, such as the Circle L engine, where the working fluid alternates between states of high and low temperatures.



In conclusion, thermal equilibrium is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that provides a framework for understanding heat transfer, entropy production, and the behavior of systems at equilibrium. It is a state of balance where no net heat flow occurs, temperatures are equal, and entropy is maximized.



### Conclusion



In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of thermodynamic equilibrium. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of systems in equilibrium, and how these principles can be applied to understand and predict the behavior of real-world systems. 



We started by defining thermodynamic equilibrium and understanding its implications. We learned that a system in thermodynamic equilibrium is in a state of maximum entropy, where all macroscopic properties are unchanging over time. We then explored the concept of equilibrium constants, which provide a quantitative measure of the position of equilibrium in a chemical reaction. 



We also discussed the laws of thermodynamics and their implications for equilibrium. We learned that the first law, which states that energy cannot be created or destroyed, implies that the total energy of a system in equilibrium is constant. The second law, which states that entropy always increases, implies that a system will naturally evolve towards a state of equilibrium. 



Finally, we discussed the concept of Gibbs free energy and its relationship with equilibrium. We learned that a reaction will proceed in the direction that decreases the Gibbs free energy of the system, and that the equilibrium position is the point at which the Gibbs free energy is at a minimum.



In conclusion, understanding thermodynamic equilibrium is crucial for predicting the behavior of systems in a wide range of fields, from chemistry and physics to engineering and environmental science. By mastering the concepts in this chapter, you are well on your way to becoming proficient in thermodynamics and kinetics.

## Chapter: Chapter 17: Thermodynamic Systems and Surroundings:



### Introduction



Thermodynamics is a fundamental branch of physics that deals with the relationships between heat, work, temperature, and energy. In essence, it is the study of the movement and conversion of energy. In this chapter, we will delve into the concept of thermodynamic systems and their surroundings, which are key components in understanding the principles of thermodynamics.



A thermodynamic system, in simple terms, is a portion of the universe that is under study. It is where we focus our attention when we want to analyze energy changes. The system can be as small as a single atom or as large as a star. Everything outside the system is referred to as the surroundings. The boundary that separates the system from its surroundings can be real or imaginary, fixed or movable, and can allow or prevent the transfer of energy or matter.



The interaction between a system and its surroundings is at the heart of thermodynamics. Energy can be transferred between the system and the surroundings in the form of heat or work. The first law of thermodynamics, also known as the law of energy conservation, states that energy cannot be created or destroyed, but can only be transferred or converted from one form to another. This law can be mathematically expressed as:


$$

\Delta U = Q - W

$$


where $\Delta U$ is the change in internal energy of the system, $Q$ is the heat transferred to the system, and $W$ is the work done by the system.



In this chapter, we will explore different types of thermodynamic systems (isolated, closed, and open systems), and how they interact with their surroundings. We will also discuss the concept of equilibrium and the role of energy transfer in reaching equilibrium. Furthermore, we will delve into the laws of thermodynamics and their implications on the behavior of thermodynamic systems.



Understanding the concepts of thermodynamic systems and surroundings is crucial in many fields, including engineering, chemistry, and environmental science. It allows us to predict how systems will respond to changes in their surroundings, and how energy transformations can be harnessed for practical applications. So, let's embark on this journey of understanding the intricate dance between thermodynamic systems and their surroundings.



### Section: 17.1 Definition of Thermodynamic Systems:



A thermodynamic system is a specific portion of the universe that is chosen for analysis. Everything outside the system is considered the surroundings. The system and surroundings are separated by a boundary which can be real or imaginary, fixed or movable, and can allow or prevent the transfer of energy or matter. 



#### 17.1a Introduction to Thermodynamic Systems



Thermodynamic systems can be classified into three types: isolated, closed, and open systems. 



- An **isolated system** is one in which neither energy nor matter can be exchanged with the surroundings. An example of an isolated system would be an insulated container, like a thermos, that does not allow the transfer of heat or matter in or out.



- A **closed system** allows the transfer of energy (heat and work) but not matter. An example of a closed system would be a sealed container of gas. The gas can expand and do work on the container by pushing it outwards, and heat can be transferred between the gas and the outside environment, but the gas itself cannot leave the container.



- An **open system** allows both energy and matter to be transferred between the system and the surroundings. An example of an open system would be a pot of boiling water. Heat can be transferred into the pot to boil the water, and steam (matter) can leave the pot and enter the surrounding environment.



The behavior of these systems is governed by the laws of thermodynamics, which describe how energy is transferred and transformed. The first law of thermodynamics, also known as the law of energy conservation, states that energy cannot be created or destroyed, but can only be transferred or converted from one form to another. This law can be mathematically expressed as:


$$

\Delta U = Q - W

$$


where $\Delta U$ is the change in internal energy of the system, $Q$ is the heat transferred to the system, and $W$ is the work done by the system.



In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the concepts of heat, work, and internal energy, and how they relate to the different types of thermodynamic systems. We will also discuss the concept of equilibrium and the role of energy transfer in reaching equilibrium. Understanding these concepts is crucial in many fields, including physics, chemistry, engineering, and environmental science.



#### 17.1b Types of Thermodynamic Systems



As mentioned in the previous section, thermodynamic systems can be classified into three types: isolated, closed, and open systems. Each of these systems has unique characteristics and obeys the laws of thermodynamics in different ways. 



##### Isolated Systems



An **isolated system** is one in which neither energy nor matter can be exchanged with the surroundings. This means that the total energy and mass within the system remain constant. The behavior of an isolated system is governed by the first law of thermodynamics, which states that the total energy of an isolated system is conserved. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:


$$

\Delta U = 0

$$


where $\Delta U$ is the change in internal energy of the system. Since no heat ($Q$) or work ($W$) is transferred in or out of an isolated system, the internal energy change is zero.



##### Closed Systems



A **closed system** allows the transfer of energy (heat and work) but not matter. This means that while the total mass within the system remains constant, the total energy can change as heat and work are transferred across the system boundary. The behavior of a closed system is also governed by the first law of thermodynamics, but unlike an isolated system, the internal energy of a closed system can change. This can be expressed as:


$$

\Delta U = Q - W

$$


where $Q$ is the heat transferred to the system, and $W$ is the work done by the system.



##### Open Systems



An **open system** allows both energy and matter to be transferred between the system and the surroundings. This means that both the total energy and mass within the system can change. The behavior of an open system is governed by both the first and second laws of thermodynamics. The first law, as applied to an open system, can be expressed as:


$$

\Delta U = Q - W + \Delta m

$$


where $\Delta m$ is the change in mass of the system due to matter transfer. The second law of thermodynamics, which states that the entropy of an isolated system can only increase, is also applicable to open systems. This law can be mathematically expressed as:


$$

\Delta S \geq 0

$$


where $\Delta S$ is the change in entropy of the system.



In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the behavior of these systems and explore how the laws of thermodynamics apply to them in different scenarios.



#### 17.1c Role in Thermodynamics



Thermodynamic systems play a crucial role in the study of thermodynamics. They serve as the primary objects of study, and their interactions with their surroundings are what drive the changes we observe in thermodynamic processes. 



##### Role of Isolated Systems



Isolated systems are particularly useful in theoretical studies and thought experiments where we want to consider a system without any external influences. For example, the concept of an isolated system allows us to understand the principle of conservation of energy, as expressed by the first law of thermodynamics. 



##### Role of Closed Systems



Closed systems, on the other hand, are more representative of real-world scenarios. Most physical systems we encounter allow energy exchange with their surroundings. For instance, a gas confined in a cylinder with a movable piston can do work on the surroundings by expanding, which is a common scenario in engines. The study of such systems allows us to understand how energy is transferred and transformed, which is crucial in the design and analysis of energy systems.



##### Role of Open Systems



Open systems are the most general type of thermodynamic system, allowing both matter and energy to be exchanged with the surroundings. These systems are representative of many biological and environmental processes. For instance, living organisms are open systems, as they exchange both energy (in the form of food and heat) and matter (in the form of gases and wastes) with their environment. The study of open systems allows us to understand these complex processes and how they are governed by the laws of thermodynamics.



In summary, the concept of thermodynamic systems is central to the study of thermodynamics. By classifying systems into isolated, closed, and open, we can better understand the different ways in which energy and matter can be transferred and transformed, and how these processes are governed by the laws of thermodynamics.



### Section: 17.2 Definition of Thermodynamic Surroundings:



#### 17.2a Introduction to Thermodynamic Surroundings



In the study of thermodynamics, the surroundings are everything outside the system of interest. The system and its surroundings together constitute the universe. The surroundings can exchange energy and matter with the system, depending on the type of system (isolated, closed, or open). 



The surroundings can be as simple as the immediate environment around the system, such as the air around a cup of hot coffee, or as complex as the entire universe outside a chemical reaction happening in a test tube. The nature of the surroundings and the way they interact with the system can significantly influence the behavior of the system.



##### Energy Exchange with Surroundings



Energy can be transferred between the system and the surroundings in the form of heat or work. Heat transfer occurs due to a temperature difference between the system and the surroundings, and it always flows from a region of higher temperature to a region of lower temperature. The general equation of heat transfer is given by:


$$

q = m c \Delta T

$$


where $q$ is the heat transferred, $m$ is the mass of the substance, $c$ is the specific heat capacity of the substance, and $\Delta T$ is the change in temperature.



Work transfer, on the other hand, occurs when a force is applied through a distance. In thermodynamics, work is often associated with the expansion or compression of gases.



##### Matter Exchange with Surroundings



In open systems, matter can also be exchanged with the surroundings. This exchange can occur through various processes, such as diffusion, convection, or advection. The rate and direction of matter exchange depend on the concentration gradients, temperature gradients, and pressure gradients between the system and the surroundings.



In summary, the thermodynamic surroundings are an integral part of any thermodynamic study. Understanding the nature of the surroundings and the ways they interact with the system is crucial for predicting and controlling thermodynamic processes.



#### 17.2b Interaction between Systems and Surroundings



The interaction between a system and its surroundings is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics. This interaction can be in the form of energy transfer, matter transfer, or both, depending on the type of system. 



##### Energy Interaction



As discussed in the previous section, energy can be transferred between the system and the surroundings in the form of heat or work. The direction of energy transfer is governed by the second law of thermodynamics, which states that energy will naturally flow from a region of higher energy to a region of lower energy. 



The total energy of the system and its surroundings is conserved, as stated by the first law of thermodynamics. This means that any energy that leaves the system must enter the surroundings, and vice versa. The equation for this energy conservation is:


$$

\Delta E_{system} + \Delta E_{surroundings} = 0

$$


where $\Delta E_{system}$ is the change in energy of the system and $\Delta E_{surroundings}$ is the change in energy of the surroundings.



##### Matter Interaction



In open systems, matter can be transferred between the system and the surroundings. This transfer can occur through various processes, such as diffusion, convection, or advection. The direction of matter transfer is typically from regions of higher concentration to regions of lower concentration, as dictated by Fick's laws of diffusion.



The rate of matter transfer can be described by the equation:


$$

J = -D \frac{\partial C}{\partial x}

$$


where $J$ is the flux (amount of substance per unit area per unit time), $D$ is the diffusion coefficient, $C$ is the concentration, and $x$ is the position.



##### System-Surroundings Boundary



The boundary between the system and its surroundings can be real or imaginary, fixed or movable, and can change in shape or size. The characteristics of the boundary can influence the type and rate of interactions between the system and the surroundings. For example, a permeable boundary allows matter and energy to be exchanged, while an impermeable boundary only allows energy exchange.



In conclusion, the interaction between a system and its surroundings is a crucial aspect of thermodynamics. Understanding these interactions allows us to predict and control the behavior of thermodynamic systems.



#### 17.2c Role in Thermodynamics



The role of the surroundings in thermodynamics is crucial as it provides the context in which a system operates. The surroundings can influence the behavior of a system and vice versa. The interactions between a system and its surroundings are the basis of thermodynamic processes.



##### Thermodynamic Equilibrium



A system is said to be in thermodynamic equilibrium with its surroundings when there is no net flow of matter or energy between them. This state is characterized by the equality of temperature, pressure, and chemical potential within the system and its surroundings. 



In the context of the equations provided, the equilibrium state can be represented as:


$$

\rho Tds + {p\over{\rho}}d\rho = 0

$$


where $\rho$ is the density, $T$ is the temperature, $ds$ is the differential of entropy, $p$ is the pressure, and $d\rho$ is the differential of density. This equation signifies that in equilibrium, the change in entropy and density of the system is zero.



##### Thermodynamic Processes



Thermodynamic processes occur when a system transitions from one state of equilibrium to another. These processes can be induced by changes in the surroundings, such as changes in temperature, pressure, or composition. 



For example, consider a gas in a piston. If the external pressure (a property of the surroundings) is increased, the gas will compress until it reaches a new state of equilibrium. This is an example of a thermodynamic process driven by changes in the surroundings.



##### Role in Entropy Production



The surroundings also play a significant role in entropy production. According to the second law of thermodynamics, the total entropy of a system and its surroundings always increases for irreversible processes. 



The equation for specific entropy production can be written as:


$$

\rho T {Ds\over{Dt}} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + {\mu\over{2}}\left( {\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}} + {\partial v_{j}\over{\partial x_{i}}} - {2\over{3}}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}

$$


where $\rho$ is the density, $T$ is the temperature, $Ds/Dt$ is the material derivative of entropy, $\kappa$ is the thermal conductivity, $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity, $v_i$ and $v_j$ are the velocity components, $x_i$ and $x_j$ are the spatial coordinates, and $\zeta$ is the bulk viscosity. This equation shows that entropy production is influenced by heat conduction, viscous dissipation, and volumetric dilatation, all of which are properties of the surroundings.



In conclusion, the surroundings play a pivotal role in thermodynamics, influencing the state and behavior of a system, driving thermodynamic processes, and contributing to entropy production.



### Section: 17.3 Thermodynamic Boundary:



#### 17.3a Definition of Thermodynamic Boundary



The thermodynamic boundary is a conceptual surface that separates a system from its surroundings. It can be real or imaginary, fixed or movable, and can change in shape or size depending on the system's state and the process it undergoes. The boundary is the interface through which energy and matter can be exchanged between the system and its surroundings.



##### Types of Thermodynamic Boundaries



There are three types of thermodynamic boundaries: 



1. **Adiabatic Boundary**: This type of boundary does not allow the transfer of heat between the system and its surroundings. However, work can be done across this boundary. An example of an adiabatic boundary is an insulated wall.



2. **Diathermic Boundary**: This type of boundary allows the transfer of heat between the system and its surroundings. Both heat and work can be transferred across this boundary. An example of a diathermic boundary is a metal wall.



3. **Rigid Boundary**: This type of boundary does not allow the system to do work on its surroundings or vice versa. However, heat can be transferred across this boundary. An example of a rigid boundary is a fixed, insulated wall.



##### Role of Thermodynamic Boundary in Thermodynamics



The thermodynamic boundary plays a crucial role in defining the system and its surroundings. It helps in understanding the interactions between the system and its surroundings, which is the basis of thermodynamics. 



The nature of the boundary (adiabatic, diathermic, or rigid) determines the type of energy (heat or work) that can be transferred between the system and its surroundings. This, in turn, influences the state and behavior of the system.



For instance, consider a gas enclosed in a piston-cylinder assembly. If the cylinder walls are adiabatic, the gas can do work on the piston (by pushing it), but it cannot exchange heat with the surroundings. If the cylinder walls are diathermic, the gas can both do work on the piston and exchange heat with the surroundings.



In the context of the equations provided, the thermodynamic boundary can be represented in the entropy production equation as:


$$

\rho T {Ds\over{Dt}} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + {\mu\over{2}}\left( {\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}} + {\partial v_{j}\over{\partial x_{i}}} - {2\over{3}}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}

$$


where $\kappa$ is the thermal conductivity, $\mu$ is the dynamic viscosity, and $\zeta$ is the bulk viscosity. This equation signifies that the rate of entropy production in a system depends on the nature of the thermodynamic boundary and the interactions across it.



he gas can both do work on the piston and exchange heat with the surroundings. If the cylinder walls are rigid, the gas cannot do work on the piston, but it can exchange heat with the surroundings.



#### 17.3b Types of Thermodynamic Boundaries



##### Permeable and Impermeable Boundaries



In addition to the adiabatic, diathermic, and rigid boundaries, thermodynamic boundaries can also be classified as permeable or impermeable. 



1. **Permeable Boundary**: This type of boundary allows the transfer of matter between the system and its surroundings. An example of a permeable boundary is a semi-permeable membrane that allows certain molecules to pass through.



2. **Impermeable Boundary**: This type of boundary does not allow the transfer of matter between the system and its surroundings. An example of an impermeable boundary is a sealed container.



##### Real and Imaginary Boundaries



Thermodynamic boundaries can also be real or imaginary. 



1. **Real Boundary**: This is a physical boundary that separates the system from its surroundings. An example of a real boundary is the wall of a container.



2. **Imaginary Boundary**: This is a conceptual boundary that we define for the purpose of analysis. An example of an imaginary boundary is the boundary we define around a specific volume of air in the atmosphere to study its thermodynamic properties.



##### Role of Different Types of Boundaries in Thermodynamics



The type of thermodynamic boundary (adiabatic, diathermic, rigid, permeable, impermeable, real, or imaginary) plays a crucial role in determining the behavior of the system and its interaction with the surroundings. 



For instance, if the boundary is adiabatic and impermeable, the system is isolated from its surroundings and its total energy (the sum of its internal energy, kinetic energy, and potential energy) remains constant. This is the basis of the first law of thermodynamics.



On the other hand, if the boundary is diathermic and permeable, the system can exchange both heat and matter with its surroundings. This can lead to changes in the system's temperature, pressure, and composition, which are described by the laws of thermodynamics.



In the next section, we will discuss the concept of thermodynamic equilibrium and how it is influenced by the type of thermodynamic boundary.



#### 17.3c Role in Thermodynamics



The thermodynamic boundary plays a significant role in thermodynamics as it defines the extent of the system and determines the type and amount of interaction the system can have with its surroundings. The nature of the boundary can significantly influence the thermodynamic processes that occur within the system.



##### Role of Thermodynamic Boundary in Heat Transfer



The thermodynamic boundary is crucial in determining the heat transfer between the system and its surroundings. For instance, a diathermic boundary allows heat transfer, while an adiabatic boundary does not. The general equation of heat transfer, $q = mc\Delta T$, where $q$ is the heat transferred, $m$ is the mass, $c$ is the specific heat capacity, and $\Delta T$ is the change in temperature, is applicable when the boundary is diathermic.



##### Role of Thermodynamic Boundary in Entropy Production



The thermodynamic boundary also plays a role in entropy production. The equation for entropy production, 


$$

\rho T \frac{Ds}{Dt} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + \frac{\mu}{2}\left( \frac{\partial v_{i}}{\partial x_{j}} + \frac{\partial v_{j}}{\partial x_{i}} - \frac{2}{3}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}

$$


shows that the entropy production depends on the heat transfer and the viscous forces, both of which are influenced by the nature of the boundary. In the case where thermal conduction and viscous forces are absent, the equation for entropy production collapses to $Ds/Dt=0$, showing that ideal fluid flow is isentropic.



##### Role of Thermodynamic Boundary in Matter Transfer



The thermodynamic boundary also determines whether matter can be transferred between the system and its surroundings. A permeable boundary allows matter transfer, while an impermeable boundary does not. This can significantly affect the thermodynamic processes within the system, such as phase changes and chemical reactions.



In conclusion, the thermodynamic boundary plays a crucial role in thermodynamics as it determines the type and amount of interaction the system can have with its surroundings. Understanding the nature of the boundary is essential for predicting and controlling the thermodynamic processes within the system.



### Conclusion



In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate world of thermodynamics and kinetics, focusing on the concepts of thermodynamic systems and surroundings. We have explored the different types of systems - isolated, closed, and open - and how they interact with their surroundings. We have also discussed the laws of thermodynamics and how they govern the behavior of these systems.



We have learned that thermodynamics is a macroscopic science that deals with energy and its transformation, while kinetics is a microscopic science that deals with the rates of chemical reactions. Both of these fields are crucial in understanding the natural phenomena around us.



The concept of thermodynamic equilibrium was also discussed, which is a state where all the properties of a system are uniform and do not change with time. We have also touched upon the concept of reversibility and irreversibility in thermodynamic processes.



In conclusion, the study of thermodynamic systems and surroundings provides a fundamental understanding of the energy transformations that occur in the universe. It is a field that has wide-ranging applications in various disciplines such as physics, chemistry, engineering, and even biology.
